reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Hamiltonian Monte Carlo (HMC) proposal using leapfrog integration.
    Simulates Hamiltonian dynamics on a batched set of particles to
    produce proposals with high acceptance probability.  The target
    distribution is a standard Gaussian for benchmarking.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim, num_leapfrog_steps=10, step_size=0.1):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.num_leapfrog_steps = num_leapfrog_steps
        self.step_size = step_size
        self.register_buffer("mu", torch.zeros(dim))

    def _grad_log_prob(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """Gradient of log p(x) for N(0, I)."""
        return -(x - self.mu)

    def _log_prob(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return -0.5 * (x ** 2).sum(dim=-1)

    def forward(self, q: torch.Tensor) -> tuple:
        """
        Args:
            q: (B, dim) – current positions
        Returns:
            (q_new, accepted):
                q_new:    (B, dim)
                accepted: (B,)
        """
        eps = self.step_size
        p = torch.randn_like(q)
        current_p = p.clone()
        current_q = q.clone()

        # Half step for momentum
        p = p + 0.5 * eps * self._grad_log_prob(q)

        for _ in range(self.num_leapfrog_steps - 1):
            q = q + eps * p
            p = p + eps * self._grad_log_prob(q)

        q = q + eps * p
        p = p + 0.5 * eps * self._grad_log_prob(q)
        p = -p  # negate for reversibility

        current_H = -self._log_prob(current_q) + 0.5 * (current_p ** 2).sum(-1)
        proposed_H = -self._log_prob(q) + 0.5 * (p ** 2).sum(-1)

        log_alpha = current_H - proposed_H
        log_u = torch.log(torch.rand(q.shape[0], device=q.device).clamp(min=1e-10))
        accepted = log_u < log_alpha
        q_new = torch.where(accepted.unsqueeze(-1), q, current_q)
        return q_new, accepted


dim = 64
batch_size = 2048


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, dim)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim]

The reference also uses `self._log_prob(x)` which is `-0.5 * (x**2).sum(dim=-1)`. The main computation is: ``` p = randn_like(q) current_p = p current_q = q p = p + 0.5 * eps * (-(q - mu)) = p - 0.5 * eps * q (since mu=0) for i in range(num_leapfrog_steps - 1): q = q + eps * p p = p + eps * (-(q - mu)) = p - eps * q q = q + eps * p p = p + 0.5 * eps * (-(q - mu)) = p - 0.5 * eps * q p = -p current_H = -(-0.5 * (current_q**2).sum(-1)) + 0.5 * (current_p**2).sum(-1) = 0.5 * (current_q**2).sum(-1) + 0.5 * (current_p**2).sum(-1) proposed_H = 0.5 * (q**2).sum(-1) + 0.5 * (p**2).sum(-1) log_alpha = current_H - proposed_H log_u = log(rand(B).clamp(min=1e-10)) accepted = log_u < log_alpha q_new = where(accepted.unsqueeze(-1), q, current_q) ``` We need to make a fast kernel. Plan for kernel: ``` __global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* q_in, const float* p_in, const float* mu, const float* log_u, float eps, int num_leapfrog_steps, int B, int dim, float* q_out, bool* accepted_out) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; // Load q and p for this batch element const float* q_ptr = q_in + b * dim; const float* p_ptr = p_in + b * dim; // We can store q, p in registers per thread. float q_val = q_ptr[tid]; float p_val = p_ptr[tid]; float mu_val = (mu != nullptr) ? mu[tid] : 0.0f; float current_q = q_val; float current_p = p_val; // Half step for momentum p_val = p_val + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q_val); // gradient = mu - q = -(q - mu) // Full steps for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) { q_val = q_val + eps * p_val; p_val = p_val + eps * (mu_val - q_val); } // Final position step q_val = q_val + eps * p_val; // Final half momentum step p_val = p_val + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q_val); // Negate momentum p_val = -p_val; // Compute sums for Hamiltonian float current_q_sq = current_q * current_q; float current_p_sq = current_p * current_p; float q_sq = q_val * q_val; float p_sq = p_val * p_val; // Reduce within block // Use warp shuffle __shared__ float sdata[...]; // We need to reduce 4 sums: current_q_sq, current_p_sq, q_sq, p_sq. // We can reduce them one by one or pack into float4. // For simplicity, reduce each. // ... float current_H = 0.5f * (sum_current_q_sq + sum_current_p_sq); float proposed_H = 0.5f * (sum_q_sq + sum_p_sq); float log_alpha = current_H - proposed_H; bool accepted = (log_u[b] < log_alpha); // Write output float* q_out_ptr = q_out + b * dim; if (accepted) { q_out_ptr[tid] = q_val; } else { q_out_ptr[tid] = current_q; } if (tid == 0) accepted_out[b] = accepted; } ``` But we need to handle the case where dim might not equal blockDim.x. However, the reduction loop with `s = blockDim.x / 2` works even for non-power-of-two as long as we don't have any elements beyond blockDim.x. Wait, blockDim.x is the number of threads. `blockDim.x / 2` using integer division. For blockDim.x=65, s=32, then 16, 8, 4, 2, 1. But what about the element at index 64? In the first iteration, tid=0 adds sdata[0] + sdata[32], tid=32 adds sdata[32] + sdata[64]. Wait, `tid < s`, so tid goes from 0 to 31. `sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + s]` adds sdata[0] += sdata[32], ..., sdata[31] += sdata[63]. Index 64 is untouched. So a simple sequential reduction by thread 0 is acceptable and simpler. Actually, even better: we can have each warp reduce its own elements using shuffle, then have thread 0 sum the warp results. Let's do the simple warp shuffle plus a small array. Generic block reduction for any block size: ``` __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) { shared[wid] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Now reduce across warps val = (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f; if (wid == 0) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } } return val; } ``` Wait, `shared` size 32 is enough because max warps per block is 32 (1024 threads). For blockDim.x=64, there are 2 warps. `wid` is 0 or 1. `shared[wid]` writes. Then we load `shared[lane]`. For lane=0,1 we get the two warp sums. For lane>=2, we get 0 (but the conditional `(threadIdx.x < num_warps)` ensures that? Actually, `threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32` is `threadIdx.x < 2`. So for threadIdx.x >= 2, val=0. Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` reads from other threads in the warp. If threads 2..31 have 0, adding them is fine. But we need to ensure the sync mask includes them. Since we use `0xFFFFFFFF`, all 32 threads participate. The first thread (lane 0) will get val from lane 16, which is 0, etc. So final sum is just sum of lane0 and lane1. This works. But there is a subtlety: `val = (threadIdx.x < num_warps) ? shared[lane] : 0.0f;` uses `lane` as the index. Since lane = threadIdx.x % 32, for threadIdx.x=0, lane=0; threadIdx.x=1, lane=1. Actually, returning to all threads is fine: after the second warp reduction, only warp 0 has the correct sum. Threads in other warps have garbage (or 0). We can broadcast the result to all threads via shared memory. Simpler: after the first reduction, write warp sums to shared. Then thread 0 reads all warp sums and adds them. ``` __inline__ __device__ float blockReduceSum(float val) { __shared__ float shared[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; // warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) shared[wid] = val; __syncthreads(); float sum = 0.0f; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; ++i) sum += shared[i]; shared[0] = sum; } __syncthreads(); return shared[0]; } ``` This is simple and robust for any blockDim.x. For 64 threads, num_warps=2, loop 2 times. Very fast. No issues with non-power-of-two. Let's use this. So the kernel: ``` __global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* __restrict__ q_in, const float* __restrict__ p_in, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ log_u, float eps, int num_leapfrog_steps, int B, int dim, float* __restrict__ q_out, bool* __restrict__ accepted_out) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; if (tid >= dim) return; // just in case block size > dim, but we'll match int offset = b * dim; float q_val = q_in[offset + tid]; float p_val = p_in[offset + tid]; float mu_val = (mu != nullptr) ? mu[tid] : 0.0f; float current_q = q_val; float current_p = p_val; // Half step for momentum p_val = p_val + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q_val); // Full leapfrog steps #pragma unroll for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) { q_val = q_val + eps * p_val; p_val = p_val + eps * (mu_val - q_val); } q_val = q_val + eps * p_val; p_val = p_val + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q_val); p_val = -p_val; // Compute partial Hamiltonians float current_H_partial = 0.5f * (current_q * current_q + current_p * current_p); float proposed_H_partial = 0.5f * (q_val * q_val + p_val * p_val); // Reduce float current_H_sum = blockReduceSum(current_H_partial); float proposed_H_sum = blockReduceSum(proposed_H_partial); bool accepted = false; if (tid == 0) { float log_alpha = current_H_sum - proposed_H_sum; accepted = (log_u[b] < log_alpha); accepted_out[b] = accepted; } // Broadcast accepted to all threads __shared__ bool shared_accepted; if (tid == 0) shared_accepted = accepted; __syncthreads(); accepted = shared_accepted; q_out[offset + tid] = accepted ? q_val : current_q; } ``` Wait, we need all threads to know `accepted` to write the output. The block reduces `current_H` and `proposed_H` but the `accepted` variable is only set in thread 0. We broadcast via shared memory. One issue: `blockReduceSum` uses `__syncthreads()` inside it. That's fine. But we can combine the two reductions into one by packing into float2 and doing a custom reduction on both simultaneously. That would halve the sync overhead. Let's implement a `blockReduceSum2` that takes two floats and reduces both. ``` __inline__ __device__ void blockReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2) { __shared__ float s1[32]; __shared__ float s2[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { v1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v1, offset); v2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, offset); } if (lane == 0) { s1[wid] = v1; s2[wid] = v2; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; float sum1 = 0.0f, sum2 = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; ++i) { sum1 += s1[i]; sum2 += s2[i]; } s1[0] = sum1; s2[0] = sum2; } __syncthreads(); v1 = s1[0]; v2 = s2[0]; } ``` This reduces both simultaneously with only 2 syncs. We'll use this. Now, the kernel code: ``` __global__ void hmc_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int offset = b * dim; float q = q_in[offset + tid]; float p = p_in[offset + tid]; float mu_val = mu ? mu[tid] : 0.0f; float q_current = q; float p_current = p; p = p + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q); for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) { q = q + eps * p; p = p + eps * (mu_val - q); } q = q + eps * p; p = p + 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q); p = -p; float H_current = 0.5f * (q_current * q_current + p_current * p_current); float H_proposed = 0.5f * (q * q + p * p); blockReduceSum2(H_current, H_proposed); bool accept = false; if (tid == 0) { float log_alpha = H_current - H_proposed; accept = log_u[b] < log_alpha; accepted_out[b] = accept; // store accept in shared memory to broadcast } __shared__ bool sh_accept; if (tid == 0) sh_accept = accept; __syncthreads(); accept = sh_accept; q_out[offset + tid] = accept ? q : q_current; } ``` Wait, we used `blockReduceSum2` which contains `__syncthreads()`. But if we want to be numerically equivalent to the reference, we must reproduce exactly what the reference does. The reference computes: ``` current_H = -self._log_prob(current_q) + 0.5 * (current_p ** 2).sum(-1) ``` Since `_log_prob(current_q) = -0.5 * (current_q ** 2).sum(dim=-1)`, then `-self._log_prob(current_q) = 0.5 * (current_q ** 2).sum(dim=-1)`. That seems like a bug in the reference, but we must match it. Wait, is that correct? In HMC, the target distribution is `p(q) \propto exp(log_prob(q))`. The potential energy is `-log_prob(q)`. If `log_prob` is for N(mu, I), it should be `-0.5 * ((q - mu)^2).sum()`. But the reference's `_log_prob` does not subtract mu. However, `_grad_log_prob` does subtract mu. Now, one more nuance: In the reference, after computing `current_H` and `proposed_H`, it computes: ``` log_alpha = current_H - proposed_H log_u = torch.log(torch.rand(q.shape[0], device=q.device).clamp(min=1e-10)) accepted = log_u < log_alpha ``` We replicate that exactly. We pass `log_u` computed in Python. Potential numerical differences: The reference computes `current_H` and `proposed_H` using PyTorch's reduction, which is done in a deterministic order. Our kernel's reduction uses warp shuffle and sequential addition by thread 0. The order of addition may differ, leading to slight fp32 differences. The tolerance is `1e-4` absolute and relative. This should be fine because the inputs are not huge, and the number of terms is small (64). The difference in summation order across 64 floats is usually within a few ULPs, well below 1e-4 for typical values. The values are of order ~1 (since q and p are standard normal), so sums are ~64. I'll write the Python code carefully. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" __inline__ __device__ void blockReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2) { __shared__ float s1[32]; Let me write a cleaner `blockReduceSum2`: ```c __inline__ __device__ void blockReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2) { __shared__ float s1[32]; __shared__ float s2[32]; int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { v1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v1, offset); v2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, offset); } if (lane == 0) { s1[wid] = v1; s2[wid] = v2; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int nw = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; float sum1 = 0.0f, sum2 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < nw; ++i) { sum1 += s1[i]; sum2 += s2[i]; } s1[0] = sum1; s2[0] = sum2; } __syncthreads(); v1 = s1[0]; v2 = s2[0]; } ``` This looks clean. Then: ```c float current_local = q_current * q_current + p_current * p_current; float proposed_local = q * q + p * p; blockReduceSum2(current_local, proposed_local); __shared__ bool sh_accept; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float current_H = 0.5f * current_local; float proposed_H = 0.5f * proposed_local; sh_accept = log_u[b] < (current_H - proposed_H); accepted_out[b] = sh_accept; } __syncthreads(); bool accept = sh_accept; q_out[offset + threadIdx.x] = accept ? q : q_current; ``` This is much cleaner! Let me write a simpler reduction using shared memory: ```c __inline__ __device__ void blockReduceSum2SM(float &v1, float &v2, int dim) { __shared__ float s1[1024]; __shared__ float s2[1024]; int tid = threadIdx.x; s1[tid] = v1; s2[tid] = v2; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride && (tid + stride) < blockDim.x && (tid + stride) < dim) { s1[tid] += s1[tid + stride]; s2[tid] += s2[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } v1 = s1[0]; v2 = s2[0]; } ``` Wait, if `blockDim.x` is not a power of 2, this stride loop won't cover all elements cleanly. A simple general tree reduction for any block size: ```c s1[tid] = (tid < dim) ? v1 : 0.0f; s2[tid] = (tid < dim) ? v2 : 0.0f; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = 1; stride < blockDim.x; stride *= 2) { int idx = tid * 2 * stride; if (idx < blockDim.x) { s1[idx] += s1[idx + stride]; s2[idx] += s2[idx + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This is the classic parallel reduction. But it's not very efficient for large block sizes because it causes bank conflicts and has divergence. However, for small block sizes (dim <= 1024), it's fine. However, the benchmark uses dim=64 which is a multiple of 32. But it's not a robust kernel. Alternatively, I can implement a safe reduction that doesn't rely on all threads in a warp: ```c __inline__ __device__ void blockReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2, int dim) { __shared__ float s1[1024]; __shared__ float s2[1024]; int tid = threadIdx.x; s1[tid] = tid < dim ? v1 : 0.0f; s2[tid] = tid < dim ? v2 : 0.0f; __syncthreads(); // Strided parallel reduction without requiring power-of-2 for (int stride = 1; stride < blockDim.x; stride *= 2) { int idx = tid * 2 * stride; if (idx + stride < blockDim.x) { s1[idx] += s1[idx + stride]; s2[idx] += s2[idx + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } v1 = s1[0]; v2 = s2[0]; } ``` Wait, this reduction pattern has the problem that multiple threads write to the same `s1[idx]`, causing race conditions. E.g., stride=1: tid=0 reads idx=0 and idx=1 and writes to s1[0]. tid=1 reads idx=2 and idx=3 and writes to s1[2]. No race. stride=2: tid=0 reads idx=0 and idx=2 and writes to s1[0]. tid=1 reads idx=4 and idx=6 and writes to s1[4]. No race. This is actually the "butterfly" reduction or "naive" reduction. It works but it's not very efficient because it leaves many threads idle in later iterations. Actually, for small arrays (<= 1024), efficiency is not critical. But a better pattern is: ```c for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s1[tid] += s1[tid + stride]; s2[tid] += s2[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This requires `blockDim.x` to be a power of 2. If I just set `blockDim.x` to the next power of 2 >= dim, then it works. For dim=64, next power of 2 is 64. For dim=100, next power of 2 is 128. For dim=1000, next power of 2 is 1024. This is perfect because it's <= 1024. Wait, if dim=1024, next power of 2 is 1024. If dim=1025, next power of 2 is 2048 > 1024, not allowed. But the benchmark uses dim=64. So I can add a check `TORCH_CHECK(dim <= 1024)` and set `int block_size = 1; while (block_size < dim) block_size <<= 1;` Then in the kernel, threads with `tid >= dim` load 0 and participate in the reduction. This is safe, efficient, and clean. The kernel uses one block per sample (B=2048), each block having threads equal to the next power of two >= dim (dim=64, so 64 threads). Also, the reference implementation computes _log_prob separately and then sums. In my kernel, I compute current_local = q_current^2 + p_current^2 and proposed_local = q^2 + p^2, then reduce and compare. This is mathematically equivalent to the reference's Hamiltonian calculation. However, let me check if the kernel is memory bound or compute bound. Since dim=64 and B=2048, we have 2048 blocks. On an Ampere/Hopper GPU, 2048 blocks should be fine. Each block uses 64 threads. Wait, looking at the code again, the reduction shared memory is `2 * block_size * sizeof(float)`. For block_size=64, that's 512 bytes per block, very small. But there's an issue: the `blockReduceSum2` function has all threads writing to shared memory, but some threads might have tid >= dim. In my kernel, if tid >= dim, q=p=0, so current_local=proposed_local=0, which is fine. Let me profile to see if there are any issues. I'll run `profile_kernel` to get diagnostics. Actually, wait. I notice the kernel currently uses `blockDim.x` for the stride size in reduction without considering dim. But the block size is a power of two >= dim, so some threads have zero values. That is fine, but we could potentially reduce block size to exactly dim (which is 64) to improve occupancy. Currently block_size = 64 because dim=64, so that's fine. Kernel code: ```cpp __global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* __restrict__ q_in, const float* __restrict__ p_in, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ u_raw, float eps, int num_leapfrog_steps, int B, int dim, float* __restrict__ q_out, bool* __restrict__ accepted_out) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int offset = b * dim; int n_pairs = dim / 2; float qx = 0.0f, qy = 0.0f, px = 0.0f, py = 0.0f; float mux = 0.0f, muy = 0.0f; if (tid < n_pairs) { int idx = offset + tid * 2; qx = q_in[idx]; qy = q_in[idx + 1]; px = p_in[idx]; py = p_in[idx + 1]; if (mu != nullptr) { mux = mu[tid * 2]; muy = mu[tid * 2 + 1]; } } float qx_cur = qx, qy_cur = qy; float px_cur = px, py_cur = py; // half step for momentum px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx); py += 0.5f * eps * (muy - qy); #pragma unroll for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) { qx += eps * px; qy += eps * py; px += eps * (mux - qx); py += eps * (muy - qy); } qx += eps * px; qy += eps * py; px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx); py += 0.5f * eps * (muy - qy); px = -px; py = -py; float current_local = 0.0f; float proposed_local = 0.0f; if (tid < n_pairs) { current_local = qx_cur*qx_cur + qy_cur*qy_cur + px_cur*px_cur + py_cur*py_cur; proposed_local = qx*qx + qy*qy + px*px + py*py; } // warp reduce (single warp if n_pairs <= 32) #pragma unroll for (int offset_shfl = 16; offset_shfl > 0; offset_shfl >>= 1) { current_local += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, current_local, offset_shfl); proposed_local += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, proposed_local, offset_shfl); } bool accept = false; if (tid == 0) { float log_alpha = 0.5f * (current_local - proposed_local); float log_u = logf(fmaxf(u_raw[b], 1e-10f)); accept = log_u < log_alpha; accepted_out[b] = accept; } accept = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, accept, 0); if (tid < n_pairs) { int idx = offset + tid * 2; q_out[idx] = accept ? qx : qx_cur; q_out[idx + 1] = accept ? qy : qy_cur; } } ``` Wait, block_size must be >= n_pairs. n_pairs = 32. This is an uncoalesced load (1 sector per request, but there are many requests). Actually, when blocks read `log_u[b]`, each block issues a separate memory request for 4 bytes. That is many requests, each taking 1 sector, but the metric is "sectors per request". That might not be the issue. But if each thread loads `log_u[b]`, then yes, within a warp, all threads load the same address. That's a broadcast, which still takes 1 sector (if cached). But if they are loaded per block, only one thread loads it. That's fine. Let's look at the `rand` and `randn` generation. In the previous kernel, `p_in` is passed from `torch.randn_like(q)`. The kernel loads `p_in[offset + tid]`. That's coalesced. `q_in[offset + tid]` is coalesced. Why would the profiler report 35.57 sectors/request? Perhaps because `block_size` was not 64. So the model is initialized with `dim=64`. Unless there's a bug in the evaluation harness. But the correctness test passed, implying the same inputs were used. But the profile says `block=832`. On H100, each SM can have up to 2048 threads. 832 threads per block doesn't divide evenly. Actually, 832 is a multiple of 32 (26 warps). Given that the profile's occupancy stats likely refer to the dominant PyTorch kernels (because they take more time), and our custom kernel's block=64 is too small to dominate the profile? No, the profile clearly says `GPU time: 19.71 μs` for our kernel, but shows `aten::clamp` as 13414 μs. The occupancy field might be a summary of the most launched kernel? Or maybe it's for our kernel if it somehow launched with block=832? That would happen if `block_size` was computed incorrectly. Let me trace `block_size` for `dim=64`: ```cpp int block_size = 1; while (block_size < dim) block_size <<= 1; // block_size = 1 -> 2 -> 4 -> 8 -> 16 -> 32 -> 64. block_size = 64. ``` The shared memory size = `2 * 64 * 4 = 512` bytes. The occupancy says `smem/blk=5760B`. That's 5760 bytes, which is `2 * 720 * 4`. So `block_size` would be 720. That is impossible with my code. So the occupancy info is definitely for another kernel (e.g., `aten::log` at 4901 μs). The profiler likely picks the kernel with the most occupancy or something. So the main issue is probably not our kernel's performance but the PyTorch overhead. However, we can trust that the evaluation system times the whole `forward` call, not just the custom kernel. So reducing PyTorch overhead is critical. Let's try these optimizations: 1. Remove `clamp` and `log` by passing raw uniform random `u` to the kernel and computing `logf(max(u, 1e-10f))` inside. 2. Remove `current_q` clone by not cloning `q` in Python; pass `q` directly and let the kernel handle reading `q_in` and storing old `q`. 3. Remove `current_p` clone similarly; we read `p_in` into registers. 4. We still need `p` from `torch.randn_like`. We can't easily remove that unless we generate in the kernel, but that breaks seeding. However, generating `p` in the kernel with `curand` while respecting PyTorch seed is possible but hard. We could instead ask PyTorch to generate `p` with `torch.randn` and pass it. Wait, I have an idea. PyTorch stores its CUDA RNG state in `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAGenerator(device)`. We can actually use `at::cuda::philox::unpack` to get the seed and offset, and then pass them to the kernel. But that's internal API and might break across PyTorch versions. Better not. So we'll keep `torch.randn_like`. But can we avoid creating a `p` tensor on GPU? We can use `torch.empty_like(q)` and then `torch.normal_(p, mean=0, std=1)`? That's the same as `torch.randn_like`. In fact, `torch.randn_like` does exactly that. But what about `torch.rand`? We can replace `torch.rand(B, device=...)` with `torch.empty(B, device=...)` and then `torch.rand_(u)`? That might be slightly faster? Probably not. Actually, we can avoid `torch.clamp` and `torch.log` entirely. Let's measure how much time that saves. Also, we can vectorize our kernel. Let's write the vectorized kernel with `float2`, using warp shuffle reduction. For dim=64, we have exactly 32 threads (one warp). This maximizes occupancy since we can have many blocks per SM. For dim not necessarily 64, we need to be careful. But since the problem's test uses dim=64, we can optimize for that. However, `ModelNew` should accept any `dim`. We can handle arbitrary `dim <= 1024` by using `int n_pairs = (dim + 1) / 2;` and setting `block_size = max(32, n_pairs)` but rounding up to multiple of 32. If `block_size > 32`, we do warp shuffle within each warp and then shared memory for inter-warp. This is a standard pattern. Let's write a robust warp-reduce + shared-memory reduce: ```cpp __device__ inline void warpReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { v1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v1, offset); v2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, offset); } } __device__ inline void blockReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2, float* smem1, float* smem2) { int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int wid = threadIdx.x / 32; warpReduceSum2(v1, v2); if (lane == 0) { smem1[wid] = v1; smem2[wid] = v2; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; float sum1 = 0.0f; float sum2 = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; ++i) { sum1 += smem1[i]; sum2 += smem2[i]; } smem1[0] = sum1; smem2[0] = sum2; } __syncthreads(); v1 = smem1[0]; v2 = smem2[0]; } ``` This is similar to before but with warp shuffle first. However, the previous version had threads that didn't participate (tid >= dim). In the shuffle version, inactive threads must have zero contribution. That's fine if they set `v1 = 0.0f`. Wait, in the previous kernel, `block_size` was a power of 2 >= dim. For `dim=64`, `block_size=64`. The block reduction used shared memory with size `2 * block_size`. The shuffle-first approach needs shared memory for `2 * num_warps`. For 64 threads, num_warps = 2, so smem = 4 floats = 16 bytes. Much less shared memory! This improves occupancy. Let's implement the shuffle-first reduction with generic `block_size` being the next multiple of 32 >= dim (or >= dim/2 for float2). For `float2` vectorized version: - `items_per_thread = 2` - `n_items = dim` - `n_threads_needed = (dim + 1) / 2` - `block_size = ((n_threads_needed + 31) / 32) * 32` - max block_size = 512 (since dim <= 1024 -> n_threads_needed <= 512) Shared memory size for reduction: `2 * (block_size/32) * sizeof(float)`. For block_size=32, that's 8 bytes. For block_size=512, that's 128 bytes. Very small. This is a huge boost in occupancy. Let's code the full kernel: ```cpp #include <cuda_runtime.h> __device__ inline void warpReduceSum2(float &v1, float &v2) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { v1 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v1, offset); v2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, v2, offset); } } __global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* __restrict__ q_in, const float* __restrict__ p_in, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ u_raw, float eps, int num_leapfrog_steps, int B, int dim, float* __restrict__ q_out, bool* __restrict__ accepted_out) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; int offset = b * dim; int n_pairs = (dim + 1) / 2; // local pair index int pair_idx = tid; float qx = 0.0f, qy = 0.0f, px = 0.0f, py = 0.0f; float mux = 0.0f, muy = 0.0f; if (pair_idx < n_pairs) { int idx = offset + pair_idx * 2; qx = q_in[idx]; px = p_in[idx]; if (mu != nullptr) mux = mu[pair_idx * 2]; if (idx + 1 < offset + dim) { qy = q_in[idx + 1]; py = p_in[idx + 1]; if (mu != nullptr) muy = mu[pair_idx * 2 + 1]; } } float qx_cur = qx, qy_cur = qy; float px_cur = px, py_cur = py; // half step px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx); py += 0.5f * eps * (muy - qy); #pragma unroll for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) { qx += eps * px; qy += eps * py; px += eps * (mux - qx); py += eps * (muy - qy); } qx += eps * px; qy += eps * py; px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx); py += 0.5f * eps * (muy - qy); px = -px; py = -py; float current_local = 0.0f; float proposed_local = 0.0f; if (pair_idx < n_pairs) { current_local = qx_cur*qx_cur + qy_cur*qy_cur + px_cur*px_cur + py_cur*py_cur; proposed_local = qx*qx + qy*qy + px*px + py*py; } // warp reduce warpReduceSum2(current_local, proposed_local); // inter-warp reduce using shared memory int lane = tid % 32; int wid = tid / 32; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem1 = smem; float* smem2 = smem + (blockDim.x / 32); if (lane == 0) { smem1[wid] = current_local; smem2[wid] = proposed_local; } __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32; float sum1 = 0.0f; float sum2 = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; ++i) { sum1 += smem1[i]; sum2 += smem2[i]; } smem1[0] = sum1; smem2[0] = sum2; } __syncthreads(); current_local = smem1[0]; proposed_local = smem2[0]; bool accept = false; if (tid == 0) { float log_alpha = 0.5f * (current_local - proposed_local); float log_u = logf(fmaxf(u_raw[b], 1e-10f)); accept = log_u < log_alpha; accepted_out[b] = accept; } accept = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, accept, 0); if (pair_idx < n_pairs) { int idx = offset + pair_idx * 2; q_out[idx] = accept ? qx : qx_cur; if (idx + 1 < offset + dim) { q_out[idx + 1] = accept ? qy : qy_cur; } } } ``` Wait, `extern __shared__ float smem[];` needs to be declared once. But we only need a small amount. Let's compute `smem_size = 2 * (block_size / 32) * sizeof(float)`. The launch function: ```cpp void launch_hmc_kernel(...) { int n_pairs = (dim + 1) / 2; int block_size = ((n_pairs + 31) / 32) * 32; if (block_size < 32) block_size = 32; dim3 grid(B); dim3 block(block_size); size_t smem_size = 2 * (block_size / 32) * sizeof(float); hmc_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(...); } ``` For dim=64: n_pairs=32, block_size=32. smem_size = 2 * 1 * 4 = 8 bytes. Each block is one warp. Occupancy is extremely high. This should be much faster. Let's also check correctness. The reference computes: `current_H = -self._log_prob(current_q) + 0.5 * (current_p ** 2).sum(-1)` `-self._log_prob(current_q)` for N(0,I) is `0.5 * (current_q ** 2).sum(-1)`. So `current_H = 0.5 * (q^2 + p^2).sum(-1)`. That's what we compute as `0.5 * current_local` where `current_local` is the sum of squares. Wait, in the kernel above, I compute `log_alpha = 0.5f * (current_local - proposed_local)`. But `current_H = 0.5 * current_local` and `proposed_H = 0.5 * proposed_local`. So `log_alpha = current_H - proposed_H = 0.5 * (current_local - proposed_local)`. Yes, correct. Note: In the original reference, `log_prob` is computed as `-0.5 * (x ** 2).sum(dim=-1)`. So `-log_prob = 0.5 * sum(x^2)`. Correct. Now let's also think about `mu`. The reference computes grad as `-(x - mu)`. For `mu=0`, it's `-x`. For general `mu`, it's `mu - x`. In our kernel: `px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx);` This corresponds to `p += 0.5 * eps * (mu - q)`. Correct. `px += eps * (mux - qx);` Inside loop, `p += eps * (mu - q)`. Correct. At the end: `px += 0.5f * eps * (mux - qx);` After the final q step, `p += 0.5 * eps * grad`. Correct. Everything looks correct. Now, for the PyTorch side: ```python def forward(self, q): B = q.shape[0] p = torch.randn_like(q) u_raw = torch.rand(B, device=q.device) q_out = torch.empty_like(q) accepted = torch.empty(B, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device) hmc.hmc_forward(q, p, self.mu, u_raw, self.step_size, self.num_leapfrog_steps, q_out, accepted) return q_out, accepted ``` This avoids `clamp` and `log`. It also avoids `current_q` and `current_p` cloning. The reference did `current_p = p.clone()` and `current_q = q.clone()`. We don't need to clone because the kernel reads the original `q_in` and `p_in` directly. This is a huge win in terms of kernel launches and memory operations. Let's code this up and test compile + correctness. Wait, I should double check the handling of `dim` odd numbers. If `dim=63`, `n_pairs=32`. The last pair (`pair_idx=31`) has `idx = offset + 31*2 = offset + 62`. `idx + 1 = offset + 63`, which is `< offset + dim` (since dim=63, offset+dim = offset+63, so idx+1 is NOT less). Wait, `idx + 1 < offset + dim` -> `offset + 62 + 1 < offset + 63` -> `offset + 63 < offset + 63` is false. So `qy` and `py` are not loaded, effectively treated as 0. That's correct. For writing back, we only write `q_out[idx+1]` if the condition holds. Good. But actually, `(dim + 1) / 2` for dim=63 is 32, but the last pair only has one element. The condition `idx + 1 < offset + dim` handles that. Good. One more thing: the `__shfl_sync` for `accept` broadcasting. In the single-warp case, `__shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, accept, 0)` works. In multi-warp case, thread 0 broadcasts to all threads in its warp, but other warps have thread 0 with uninitialized `accept` variable? Actually, `accept` is set to `false` initially. Only `tid==0` sets it to the computed value. Then `__shfl_sync` from lane 0 broadcasts within warp 0. But what about threads in other warps? Their `accept` is still `false`! We need to broadcast to all warps. We can use shared memory for that. So: ```cpp __shared__ bool sh_accept; if (tid == 0) { ... sh_accept = accept; } __syncthreads(); accept = sh_accept; ``` This is safer and works for any block size. Let's use that instead of `__shfl_sync`. Revised kernel: ```cpp __global__ void hmc_kernel(...) { // ... compute ... __shared__ bool sh_accept; if (tid == 0) { float log_alpha = 0.5f * (current_local - proposed_local); float log_u = logf(fmaxf(u_raw[b], 1e-10f)); sh_accept = log_u < log_alpha; accepted_out[b] = sh_accept; } __syncthreads(); bool accept = sh_accept; // ... write back ... } ``` Great. One more optimization: In the original code, `current_H` and `proposed_H` are computed using `self._log_prob(q)` and `(p**2).sum(-1)`. Our kernel computes them correctly. What about `torch.where(accepted.unsqueeze(-1), q, current_q)`? Our kernel does `accept ? q : q_current` per element. Correct. Let's make sure `accepted_out` is correctly typed. We declared `bool* accepted_out`. In PyTorch, `torch.empty(B, dtype=torch.bool)` gives a `uint8` or `bool` tensor. The CUDA `bool` is 1 byte. In ATen, `at::Tensor` with `kBool` uses `bool`? Actually, `data_ptr<bool>()` should work. Let's check: `accepted.data_ptr<bool>()` is used. In the C++ API, `bool` is fine. One more thing: `u_raw` is `torch.rand(B, device=q.device)`. Should we ensure it's contiguous? `torch.rand` creates a contiguous tensor by default. Also, `mu` might be on CPU? `self.register_buffer("mu", torch.zeros(dim))`. Since `q` is on CUDA, `self.mu` will be moved to CUDA if the model is moved to CUDA. The evaluation harness likely moves the model to CUDA. But to be safe, in the Python forward, we can pass `self.mu` directly. In the C++ wrapper, we check if `mu` is defined, on CUDA, and contiguous. What if `mu` is on CPU? The reference would crash if `q` is on CUDA and `mu` is on CPU, because `-(x - self.mu)` would try to do CPU-CUDA cross-device operation. So `self.mu` must be on the same device as `q`. We don't need to handle CPU case. Let's compile this.

Wait, in the kernel each thread does: ``` qx = q_in[idx]; if (idx + 1 < ...) qy = q_in[idx + 1]; ``` The issue could be that the compiler generates separate load instructions for the two elements, but warp-level coalescing happens per instruction. For `q_in[idx]`, threads 0..31 in a warp read elements 0,2,4,...,62 (strided by 2). This is NOT contiguous; it's a stride-2 access pattern! This is strided by 2, so only 50% utilization per cache line (since each 128-byte line holds 32 floats, but we only read the even ones). Similarly for `qy` we read the odd ones in a separate instruction. So for each of the 4 loads (q_in even, q_in odd, p_in even, p_in odd), we have a stride-2 pattern. warpReduceSum(float& val) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset);\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* unroll\n for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) {\n q += eps * p;\n p += eps * (mu_val - q);\n }\n\n q += eps * p;\n p += 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q);\n p = -p;\n\n float ++i) {\n sum_cur += smem_cur[i];\n sum_prop += smem_prop[i];\n }\n smem_cur[0] = sum_cur;\n smem_prop[0] = sum_prop;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n __shared__ bool sh_accept;\n if (tid == 0) {\n float log_alpha = 0.5f * (smem_cur[0] - smem_prop[0]);\n float log_u = logf(fmaxf(u_raw[b], 1e-10f));\n sh_accept = log_u < log_alpha;\n accepted_out[b] = sh_accept;\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n q_out[offset + tid] = sh_accept ? q : q_cur;\n}\n\nvoid launch_hmc_kernel(const float* q_in,\n const float* p_in,\n const float* mu,\n const float* u_raw,\n float eps,\n int num_leapfrog_steps,\n int B,\n int 32) * sizeof(float);\n hmc_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(q_in, p_in, mu, u_raw,\n eps, num_leapfrog_steps,\n B, dim, q_out, accepted_out);\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include 1 && accepted.size(0) == B, \"accepted shape mismatch\");\n const float* mu_ptr = nullptr;\n if (mu.defined() && mu.numel() > 0) {\n TORCH_CHECK(mu.is_cuda(), \"mu must be CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(mu.is_contiguous(), \"mu must be contiguous\");\n mu_ptr = mu.data_ptr<float>();\n }\n launch_hmc_kernel(q.data_ptr<float>(),\n p.data_ptr<float>(),\n mu_ptr,\n u_raw.data_ptr<float>(),\n warpReduceSum(float& val) {\n #pragma unroll\n for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {\n val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset);\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* * q_cur + p_cur * p_cur;\n float proposed_local = q * q + p * p;\n\n warpReduceSum(current_local);\n warpReduceSum(proposed_local);\n\n int lane = tid % 32;\n int wid = tid / 32;\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_cur = smem;\n float* smem_prop = smem + (blockDim.x / 32);\n\n if (lane == 0) {\n smem_cur[wid] = current_local;\n smem_prop[wid] = proposed_local;\n }\n import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__device__ inline void warpReduceSum(float& val) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) {
        val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset);
    }
}

__global__ void hmc_kernel(const float* __restrict__ q_in,
                           const float* __restrict__ p_in,
                           const float* __restrict__ mu,
                           const float* __restrict__ u_raw,
                           float eps,
                           int num_leapfrog_steps,
                           int B,
                           int dim,
                           float* __restrict__ q_out,
                           bool* __restrict__ accepted_out) {
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    if (b >= B) return;
    if (tid >= dim) return;
    int offset = b * dim;
    
    float q = __ldg(q_in + offset + tid);
    float p = __ldg(p_in + offset + tid);
    float mu_val = (mu != nullptr) ? __ldg(mu + tid) : 0.0f;
    float q_cur = q;
    float p_cur = p;

    p += 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q);

    #pragma unroll
    for (int step = 0; step < num_leapfrog_steps - 1; ++step) {
        q += eps * p;
        p += eps * (mu_val - q);
    }

    q += eps * p;
    p += 0.5f * eps * (mu_val - q);
    p = -p;

    float current_local = q_cur * q_cur + p_cur * p_cur;
    float proposed_local = q * q + p * p;

    warpReduceSum(current_local);
    warpReduceSum(proposed_local);

    int lane = tid % 32;
    int wid = tid / 32;
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* smem_cur = smem;
    float* smem_prop = smem + (blockDim.x / 32);

    if (lane == 0) {
        smem_cur[wid] = current_local;
        smem_prop[wid] = proposed_local;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    if (tid == 0) {
        int num_warps = (blockDim.x + 31) / 32;
        float sum_cur = 0.0f;
        float sum_prop = 0.0f;
        #pragma unroll
        for (int i = 0; i < num_warps; ++i) {
            sum_cur += smem_cur[i];
            sum_prop += smem_prop[i];
        }
        smem_cur[0] = sum_cur;
        smem_prop[0] = sum_prop;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    __shared__ bool sh_accept;
    if (tid == 0) {
        float log_alpha = 0.5f * (smem_cur[0] - smem_prop[0]);
        float log_u = logf(fmaxf(__ldg(u_raw + b), 1e-10f));
        sh_accept = log_u < log_alpha;
        accepted_out[b] = sh_accept;
    }
    __syncthreads();

    q_out[offset + tid] = sh_accept ? q : q_cur;
}

void launch_hmc_kernel(const float* q_in,
                       const float* p_in,
                       const float* mu,
                       const float* u_raw,
                       float eps,
                       int num_leapfrog_steps,
                       int B,
                       int dim,
                       float* q_out,
                       bool* accepted_out) {
    int block_size = dim;
    if (block_size > 1024) block_size = 1024;
    dim3 grid(B);
    dim3 block(block_size);
    size_t smem_size = 2 * ((block_size + 31) / 32) * sizeof(float);
    hmc_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>(q_in, p_in, mu, u_raw,
                                           eps, num_leapfrog_steps,
                                           B, dim, q_out, accepted_out);
}
'''

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>

void launch_hmc_kernel(const float* q_in,
                       const float* p_in,
                       const float* mu,
                       const float* u_raw,
                       float eps,
                       int num_leapfrog_steps,
                       int B,
                       int dim,
                       float* q_out,
                       bool* accepted_out);

void hmc_forward(at::Tensor q,
                 at::Tensor p,
                 at::Tensor mu,
                 at::Tensor u_raw,
                 float eps,
                 int num_leapfrog_steps,
                 at::Tensor q_out,
                 at::Tensor accepted) {
    TORCH_CHECK(q.is_cuda(), "q must be CUDA");
    TORCH_CHECK(p.is_cuda(), "p must be CUDA");
    TORCH_CHECK(u_raw.is_cuda(), "u_raw must be CUDA");
    TORCH_CHECK(q.is_contiguous(), "q must be contiguous");
    TORCH_CHECK(p.is_contiguous(), "p must be contiguous");
    TORCH_CHECK(q_out.is_contiguous(), "q_out must be contiguous");
    TORCH_CHECK(u_raw.is_contiguous(), "u_raw must be contiguous");
    TORCH_CHECK(q.dim() == 2, "q must be 2D");
    int B = q.size(0);
    int dim = q.size(1);
    TORCH_CHECK(dim <= 1024, "dim must be <= 1024");
    TORCH_CHECK(p.sizes() == q.sizes(), "p shape mismatch");
    TORCH_CHECK(u_raw.dim() == 1 && u_raw.size(0) == B, "u_raw shape mismatch");
    TORCH_CHECK(q_out.sizes() == q.sizes(), "q_out shape mismatch");
    TORCH_CHECK(accepted.dim() == 1 && accepted.size(0) == B, "accepted shape mismatch");
    const float* mu_ptr = nullptr;
    if (mu.defined() && mu.numel() > 0) {
        TORCH_CHECK(mu.is_cuda(), "mu must be CUDA");
        TORCH_CHECK(mu.is_contiguous(), "mu must be contiguous");
        mu_ptr = mu.data_ptr<float>();
    }
    launch_hmc_kernel(q.data_ptr<float>(),
                      p.data_ptr<float>(),
                      mu_ptr,
                      u_raw.data_ptr<float>(),
                      eps,
                      num_leapfrog_steps,
                      B,
                      dim,
                      q_out.data_ptr<float>(),
                      accepted.data_ptr<bool>());
}
'''

hmc = load_inline(
    name="hmc_opt_v3",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["hmc_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, dim, num_leapfrog_steps=10, step_size=0.1):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.num_leapfrog_steps = num_leapfrog_steps
        self.step_size = step_size
        self.register_buffer("mu", torch.zeros(dim))

    def forward(self, q):
        B = q.shape[0]
        p = torch.randn_like(q)
        u_raw = torch.rand(B, device=q.device)
        q_out = torch.empty_like(q)
        accepted = torch.empty(B, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device)
        hmc.hmc_forward(
            q, p, self.mu, u_raw,
            self.step_size, self.num_leapfrog_steps,
            q_out, accepted
        )
        return q_out, accepted
